Hong Kong: Context (1980-1990)

Governmental Regulations on Religion and Legal Cases

Hong Kong

Historical Period: Before 1990 (with a focus on the decade from 1980 to 1989. As background, briefly mention significant laws and events from earlier periods, particularly those that had a major impact on the circumstances of this decade.)

Researcher Name: Xing Fuzeng

Social Context Includes Three Main Aspects:

  1. Government regulations and legal cases related to religion

    1. Social hostility and actions targeting Christianity

      1. Geopolitical environment and diplomatic relations

          2. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´ „ÄåÊñáÁçª „Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†Áõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê

          1. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´ „ÄåÊñáÁçª „Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†Áõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê

        ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

        1. The Executive Council‚Äôs Policy on Land Use by Religious Organizations (1981)

          (References: Christian Weekly 1981; Message 1981; Christian Times 1996)

          In 1973, Governor Murray MacLehose launched the New Town Development Plan to address rapid population growth. Tsuen Wan, Sha Tin, and Tuen Mun were the first new towns, followed by Tai Po, Fanling, Sheung Shui, and Yuen Long in the late 1970s as the second-generation new towns. When the Hong Kong Christian Council reviewed the planning blueprint for Sha Tin New Town, they discovered no designated ‚Äúreligious land use‚Äù areas. Churches could only acquire land at market prices, making it difficult to afford land for building places of worship due to rising property prices.

          The Hong Kong Christian Council, along with the Catholic Diocese of Hong Kong, engaged in multiple negotiations with the government. In 1979, Rev. Kwok Nai Hung (Secretary-General of the Christian Council), Bishop John Gilbert Hind of the Anglican Church, and Cardinal John Baptist Wu of the Catholic Church reached an agreement with Chief Secretary Jack Cater. In March 1981, the Hong Kong government drafted a new policy proposal and consulted with religious organizations. On May 12, the Executive Council officially approved the policy, which allowed religious organizations providing social services to use social service centers for divine worship or clergy residences at no premium under the following conditions:

          1. Religious activities must occupy no more than one floor, and the space must also serve community activities compatible with the religious functions.

            1. Clergy residences must not exceed 200 square meters and should relate directly to the social service activities of the center.

              Additionally, the Executive Council permitted religious organizations to use subsidized school halls for religious purposes. This policy enabled many churches in the 1980s to expand rapidly in new towns by establishing social services and schools.

              ‚Äî

              1. Code of Aid for Secondary Schools and Primary Schools (1983)

                The 1983 Code of Aid for Secondary Schools and Code of Aid for Primary Schools included two provisions regarding religion:

                1. Religious education time was limited to 1.5 hours per week. Any additional time could not be counted as part of the regular curriculum.

                  1. Schools were prohibited from compelling students to receive religious instruction or participate in religious rituals and were required to provide alternative arrangements for those who opted out. Students had to be informed of this rule at the beginning of each academic year.

                    ‚Äî

                    1. Provisions on Religion in the Sino-British Joint Declaration (1984)

                      (Joint Declaration, 1984)

                      In 1984, the governments of China and the United Kingdom reached an agreement on Hong Kong‚Äôs future after 1997 and signed the Joint Declaration. Article 3 outlined China‚Äôs basic policies for Hong Kong, stating that the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region would safeguard freedoms and rights, including those of religion. Annex I further specified:

                      • Religious organizations and believers could maintain ties with counterparts elsewhere. Religious schools, hospitals, and welfare institutions could continue to operate.

                        • Relations between Hong Kong‚Äôs religious organizations and their counterparts in mainland China would be based on mutual independence, non-interference, and mutual respect (the ‚Äúthree mutual principles‚Äù).

                          • Educational autonomy for institutions, including those run by religious and social organizations, would be preserved, and they could recruit staff and select teaching materials freely.

                            These provisions established basic religious freedoms for Hong Kong after 1997, ensuring that religious organizations could maintain international connections, continue operating educational and welfare services, and enjoy institutional autonomy.

                            ‚Äî

                            1. Drafting and Consultation of the Basic Law and Christianity (1985)

                              (Xing Fuzeng, 2018: 207-209)

                              In September 1984, the Sino-British Joint Declaration was signed, affirming China‚Äôs sovereignty over Hong Kong from July 1, 1997. Following the declaration, China launched the drafting of the Basic Law, establishing a Drafting Committee and a Consultative Committee in 1985, which included representatives from the religious community.

                              The Anglican Bishop Peter Kwong and Buddhist leader Sik Kok Kwong were among the drafters. Bishop Kwong, upon joining the drafting committee, expressed his focus on issues such as religious freedom and the continuation of religious participation in social and educational services. The six major religions in Hong Kong appointed representatives to the consultative committee, providing a platform for religious perspectives on the Basic Law.

                              Recognizing the need for an independent Christian voice, the Hong Kong Christian Council established the ‚ÄúHong Kong Christian Concern for the Basic Law Committee‚Äù in 1984, offering a unified platform for diverse denominations to address issues arising from the drafting process.

                              ‚Äî

                              1. Should the Basic Law Include Provisions on ‚ÄúReligious Policy‚Äù? (1986-1987)

                                (Xing Fuzeng, 2018: 246-248)

                                In April 1986, the second plenary meeting of the Basic Law Drafting Committee proposed adding ‚ÄúReligious Policy‚Äù to Chapter VI of the outline. This sparked concerns within Hong Kong‚Äôs religious community. Some argued that having a ‚Äúreligious policy‚Äù was characteristic of authoritarian states, leading to fears of potential government interference in religious affairs.

                                While some Christian leaders advocated for retaining existing ‚Äúprivileges‚Äù granted to religious organizations under colonial policies, others emphasized that constitutional guarantees of religious freedom should not include administrative privileges. In 1987, after extensive debate, the drafting committee decided to remove the controversial ‚Äúreligious policy‚Äù term, opting instead for ‚Äúreligious issues.‚Äù

                                This controversy highlighted differing opinions on the relationship between religion and government in post-1997 Hong Kong. It reflected the tension between maintaining colonial-era privileges for religious organizations and upholding the principle of religious freedom without administrative favoritism.

                                1. Controversy Over the Christian Delegation‚Äôs Visit to Beijing

                                  (Xing Fuzeng, 2018: 180-185)

                                  The planned visit of a Hong Kong Christian delegation to Beijing from September 5 to 9 sparked significant attention within the church community.

                                  Rev. Lam Yu Shing acknowledged in Christian Weekly that ‚Äúsome believers remain entangled in past nightmares,‚Äù adding, ‚Äúwhile various groups are organizing visits to Beijing, some church leaders hesitate.‚Äù Upon returning to Hong Kong, Yu Dat San shared his observations and emotions in writing. He candidly noted: ‚ÄúMany brothers and sisters strongly oppose any interaction or dialogue between Hong Kong churches and the Chinese Communist government out of fear of becoming targets of united front strategies. From historical lessons, the term ‚Äòunited front‚Äô undoubtedly evokes unease and concern. No wonder some believers tremble at its mention.‚Äù He further pointed out: ‚ÄúFor some members of our delegation, accepting this invitation was no easy decision. Concerns ranged from doubts about the effectiveness of the visit to fears that the delegation would become a tool for the Religious Affairs Bureau and the Three-Self Patriotic Movement. A bigger worry was the potential rejection of this action by church members.‚Äù

                                  To address these concerns, evangelical members of the delegation organized a sharing session on August 18 at Hebron Church. Prominent figures such as Rev. Stephen Tong, Philip Chien (Student Evangelical Fellowship), and Ernest Choi (Breakthrough) expressed their views. The Olive Tree noted particular interest in Rev. Tong, who rarely commented on Chinese politics and church issues. At the session, some attendees questioned the ‚Äúunited front‚Äù implications of the visit and whether it might undermine the diversity within Hong Kong churches. These issues were addressed by the delegation members.

                                  Philip Chien emphasized the need for trust and spiritual support from the church: ‚ÄúWe need courage, wisdom, and the Holy Spirit‚Äôs guidance to make this visit a genuine opportunity for dialogue between the church and the government.‚Äù Ernest Choi admitted his initial hesitation upon receiving the invitation, describing his inner conflict.

                                  Rev. Tong highlighted his belief in God‚Äôs sovereignty over history, stating that the church must adapt wisely to changes. He shared that, upon receiving the invitation, he felt compelled to voice the church‚Äôs stance on religious freedom, saying, ‚ÄúHong Kong citizens are expressing their views on Hong Kong‚Äôs future; the Christian community also has a responsibility to speak out.‚Äù He dismissed accusations of futility, emphasizing a dynamic perspective on China‚Äôs evolving policies. He acknowledged the potential for practical religious freedom in Hong Kong under the Chinese Communist Party‚Äôs shifting economic policies.

                                  Rev. Tong‚Äôs decision to accept the invitation marked a significant departure from his typically reserved approach to Chinese and political matters. However, on the day of departure, he dramatically withdrew from the trip, reflecting the immense pressures he faced.

                                  At the time, many Hong Kong and North American Chinese churches maintained a ‚Äúno reconciliation‚Äù stance toward the Chinese Communist Party and the Three-Self Movement, branding any interaction as betrayal. For instance, North American pastor Wang Guoxian warned of potential infiltration by Communist Party members like Li Chuwen, who had posed as an underground church member. Wang criticized the delegation, asserting that it was a political tool to placate Hong Kong citizens and project a facade of openness internationally.

                                  ‚Äî

                                  1. Controversy Over Preferential Hiring of Christian Teachers in Government-Subsidized Schools

                                    Starting in the 1970s, the Hong Kong government significantly invested in education to address shortages of school places, implementing six and nine years of free education in 1971 and 1978, respectively. Many Christian organizations accepted government subsidies to establish schools, incorporating Bible studies into their curricula. Evangelical organizations often prioritized evangelism among students, leading to job advertisements favoring Christian applicants.

                                    By the 1980s, this hiring practice drew criticism, with claims that it violated the principles of public fund usage. In 1986, the South China Morning Post published a letter alleging discriminatory practices during interviews, such as questioning applicants‚Äô biblical knowledge and church involvement. Critics argued this amounted to discrimination against non-Christians.

                                    In response, leaders like Cheung Tsz Kwong defended the practice, emphasizing the freedom of choice in Hong Kong‚Äôs diverse educational landscape. He argued that schools with religious missions required teachers who shared their faith to uphold their values. Critics countered that using public funds for religious purposes was discriminatory and incompatible with public accountability.

                                    In 1996, the establishment of the Equal Opportunities Commission led to regulations prohibiting discriminatory language like ‚ÄúChristian preferred‚Äù in job advertisements.

                                    ‚Äî

                                    1. Controversy Over The Last Temptation of Christ (1988)

                                      (Christian Weekly, 1988; Wah Kiu Yat Po, 1988; Ta Kung Pao, 1988; Liu Dafa, 1988)

                                      In November 1988, the film The Last Temptation of Christ was classified as Category III (restricted to adults over 18) and screened in Hong Kong. Due to its portrayal of Jesus facing human temptations, the film provoked widespread controversy among Christian communities globally.

                                      On November 7, the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union sent letters to the Chief Secretary‚Äôs Office, legislative council members, and the Film Censorship Office, requesting a ban on the film to protect the sanctity of Christ‚Äôs image. The Union also distributed letter templates to 249 member churches, urging them to petition the authorities. A special task force was formed, declaring, ‚ÄúHong Kong believers must not remain silent in defending the dignity of faith.‚Äù

                                      While many religious groups joined the call for a ban, others advocated a rational response, emphasizing freedom of speech and artistic expression in Hong Kong. Outside a Wan Chai theater, about 20 church members protested, distributing leaflets urging the public not to watch the film.

                                      This incident highlighted tensions between religious advocacy and freedom of expression, reflecting broader debates about moral and social values in Hong Kong.

                                      1. The ‚Äú1997‚Äù Question and 1980s Hong Kong: Sino-British Power Struggles

                                        (Carroll 2007: 179‚Äì187; Xu Cheng‚Äôen 2023: 246‚Äì254; Liu Shuyong 2016: 319‚Äì323)

                                        In 1980s Hong Kong, the question of ‚Äú1997‚Äù dominated the decade, centering on Sino-British negotiations, agreements over Hong Kong‚Äôs future, and the power struggle regarding arrangements during the transitional period before the promulgation of the Basic Law in 1991. Events such as the 1989 Beijing Student Movement and the subsequent Tiananmen Square Massacre further reshaped the triangular relationship between China, Britain, and Hong Kong.

                                        In 1979, Governor Murray MacLehose visited Beijing and learned of China‚Äôs decision to reclaim Hong Kong in 1997. Britain responded with preparations:

                                        1. In 1980, the Hong Kong government issued the Green Paper on Local Administration, followed by the White Paper on Local Administration and the District Boards Ordinance in 1981. These measures led to the first District Board elections in 1982 and Municipal Council elections in 1983.

                                          1. The British Nationality Act 1981 stripped Hong Kong residents of their right of abode in the UK.

                                            Although Sino-British negotiations on Hong Kong‚Äôs future had not formally begun, Britain‚Äôs reforms in local governance during the postwar period were likely intended to pave the way for representative democracy.

                                            In September 1982, British Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher visited China, initiating official negotiations on Hong Kong‚Äôs future. China insisted that the talks were a Sino-British matter, excluding Hong Kong‚Äôs direct participation. Britain‚Äôs proposal for ‚Äúsovereignty for administration‚Äù was rejected by China, which refused to recognize the Treaty of Nanking (1842), the Convention of Peking (1860), or the Convention for the Extension of Hong Kong Territory (1898). By late 1983, China warned that it would unilaterally announce plans to reclaim Hong Kong if no agreement was reached by October 1, 1984, pressuring Britain to shift its focus from sovereignty to ensuring stability post-handover.

                                            In September 1984, representatives from China and Britain reached an agreement and drafted the Sino-British Joint Declaration, which was formally signed in December by Thatcher and Chinese Premier Zhao Ziyang. The declaration stipulated that Hong Kong would be returned to China on July 1, 1997, and a Special Administrative Region (SAR) would be established with a high degree of autonomy, excluding foreign affairs and defense. Hong Kong‚Äôs existing social and economic systems and lifestyle would remain unchanged.

                                            In 1985, following the exchange of ratifications for the Joint Declaration, Hong Kong officially entered its transitional period, with preparations for the 1997 handover, including the drafting of the Basic Law. The drafting committee, established in June 1985, included members from China and Hong Kong, and a consultative committee was set up in December of the same year. Although China would officially reclaim sovereignty in 1997, Chinese influence was already apparent during the transitional period, with Beijing launching united front campaigns targeting various sectors in Hong Kong. The business community, playing the role of ‚Äúlocal collaborators,‚Äù emerged as a counterforce to the pro-democracy camp.

                                            During the late stages of the Sino-British negotiations, the Hong Kong government released the Green Paper on Representative Government in 1984, proposing a gradual move towards a system grounded in public opinion and accountability to Hong Kong residents. The subsequent White Paper proposed 24 indirectly elected members for the Legislative Council in 1985, reducing appointed and ex officio members to 22 and 19, respectively. It also mentioned the possibility of introducing directly elected seats in 1988. However, China opposed accelerated democratic reforms, arguing that the transitional period‚Äôs political system must align with the Basic Law.

                                            Under pressure from China, Britain agreed to avoid major political reforms before the promulgation of the Basic Law in 1990. In 1987, the Hong Kong government‚Äôs Green Paper reviewed the development of representative government but deferred the introduction of directly elected seats to 1991 due to societal divisions and concerns about smooth power transitions in 1997.

                                            Throughout the negotiations, Hong Kong‚Äôs voice was largely absent. While many Hong Kong residents preferred British rule post-1997 due to distrust in the Chinese Communist Party, fears of instability similar to post-1949 mainland China fueled skepticism. The 1983 devaluation of the Hong Kong dollar amid stalled Sino-British negotiations and Jardine Matheson‚Äôs relocation to Bermuda in 1984 underscored the uncertainty.

                                            From the onset of Sino-British negotiations to the signing of the Joint Declaration, a wave of emigration swept through Hong Kong. From 1980 to 1986, approximately 20,000 residents emigrated annually. The Tiananmen Square Massacre in 1989 further accelerated this trend, peaking in subsequent years. The 1994 publication of Reluctant Exiles? by the University of Hong Kong explored this emigration phenomenon, highlighting different perspectives through its Chinese title, ‚Äú‰ΩïÂúüÂêæÈÑâ?‚Äù (Where is My Home?).

                                            1980Âπ¥‰ª£ÁöÑÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÔºå„Äå‰πù‰∏É„ÄçÁÑ°ÁñëÊòØÊï¥ÂÄãÂçÅÂπ¥ÁöÑÈóúÈçµË©ûÔºåËÉåÂæåÊ∂âÂèä‰∫Ü‰∏≠Ëã±ÂÖ©ÂúãÂ∞±È¶ôÊ∏ØÂâçÈÄîÂïèÈ°åÁöÑË´áÂà§„ÄÅÂçîË≠∞Ôºå‰ª•ÂèäÂú®Âü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÈ†í‰ΩàÔºà1991ÔºâÂâç‰∏≠Ëã±Â∞±È¶ôÊ∏ØÈÅéÊ∏°ÊúüÂÆâÊéíÁöÑËßíÂäõ„ÄÇÂÖ∂‰∏≠1989Âπ¥Âåó‰∫¨Â≠∏ÈÅãÁöÑÁàÜÁôºÔºå‰ª•Âèä„ÄåÂÖ≠Âõõ„ÄçÂ±†ÊÆ∫ÊÖòÊ°àÂâçÂæåÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁ§æÊúÉÂ∞ç‰∏≠ÂúãÊ∞ë‰∏ªÈÅãÂãïÁöÑÊîØÊè¥Ôºå‰πüÊää‰∏≠Ëã±Ê∏ØÁöÑ‰∏âËßíÈóú‰øÇÊé®ÂêëÊñ∞ÁöÑÊ†ºÂ±Ä„ÄÇ

                                            1979Âπ¥Ê∏ØÁù£È∫•ÁêÜÊµ©Ë®™ÂïèÂåó‰∫¨ÔºåÁü•ÊÇâ‰∏≠ÂÖ±Âú®1997Âπ¥Êî∂ÂõûÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁöÑÊ±∫ÂÆöÂæåÔºåËã±ÂúãÈñãÂßã‰ΩúÂá∫ÈÉ®ÁΩ≤ÔºöÔºà1Ôºâ1980Âπ¥ÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊîøÂ∫úÁôºË°®„ÄäÂú∞ÊñπË°åÊîøÊ®°ÂºèÁ∂†ÁöÆÊõ∏„ÄãÔºåÁøåÂπ¥ÂÖ¨‰Ωà„ÄäÂú∞ÊñπË°åÊîøÁôΩÁöÆÊõ∏„ÄãÂíå„ÄäÂçÄË≠∞ÊúÉÊ¢ù‰æã„ÄãÔºå‰∏¶ÊìöÊ≠§Êñº1982Âπ¥ËàâË°åÈ¶ñÂ±ÜÂçÄË≠∞ÊúÉÈÅ∏Ëàâ„ÄÇ1983Âπ¥ÔºåÂ∏ÇÊîøÂ±Ä‰∫¶ËàâË°åÂàÜÂçÄÈÅ∏ËàâÔºõÔºà2Ôºâ1981Âπ¥ÈÄöÈÅé„ÄäËã±ÂúãÂúãÁ±çÊ≥ï„ÄãÔºåÂâùÂ•™È¶ôÊ∏ØÂ±ÖÊ∞ëÁöÑÂ±ÖËã±Ê¨äÂà©„ÄÇÊòØÊôÇÔºå‰∏≠Ëã±ÂÖ©Âúã‰ªçÊú™Ê≠£ÂºèÂ∞±È¶ôÊ∏ØÂâçÈÄîÂïèÈ°åÈÄ≤Ë°åÁ£ãÂïÜÔºå‰ΩÜËã±ÂúãÂçªËëóÊâãÊé®Ë°åÊà∞ÂæåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂú∞ÊñπË°åÊîøÁöÑÈáçÂ§ßÊîπÈù©ÔºåÁõ∏‰ø°ÊòØÁÇ∫Êó•ÂæåÁôºÂ±ï‰ª£Ë≠∞ÊîøÂà∂‰ΩúÈ†êÂÇô„ÄÇ

                                            Ëá™ÂßãËá≥ÁµÇÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰πù‰∏ÉË´áÂà§ÂèäÂçîË≠∞ÂùáÁî±‰∏≠Ëã±ÂÖ©ÂúãÊâÄ‰∏ªÂ∞éÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∫∫ÁöÑÊÑèË¶ãÁÑ°Ê≥ïÁõ¥Êé•Âú®Ë´áÂà§‰∏ä‰∫à‰ª•Ë°®ÈÅî„ÄÇÈù¢Â∞çÂâçÈÄî„ÄåÂ§ßÈôê„ÄçÔºåÂç≥‰ΩøËã±ÂúãÂú®1981Âπ¥ÈÄöÈÅé„ÄäËã±ÂúãÂúãÁ±çÊ≥ï„ÄãÔºåÊ∏Ø‰∫∫‰ªçÊôÆÈÅçÊúüÊúõ‰πù‰∏ÉÂπ¥ÂæåÁπºÁ∫åÊé•ÂèóËã±ÂúãÁÆ°Ê≤ª„ÄÇ‰∏ªË¶ÅÂéüÂõ†ÔºåÁÑ°ÁñëÊòØÂ∞ç‰∏≠ÂúãÂÖ±Áî¢Èª®Áº∫‰πè‰ø°ÂøÉÔºåÊìîÂøÉ1949Âπ¥‰∏≠ÂÖ±Âª∫ÂúãÂæåÁöÑÂãïÁõ™ÔºåÊúÉÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÈáçÊºî„ÄÇ„Äç1983Âπ¥9ÊúàÔºåÁî±Êñº‰∏≠Ëã±Ë´áÂà§Ê≤íÊúâÈÄ≤Â±ïÔºåÊ∏ØÂÖÉÂåØÂÉπÊÄ•Âäá‰∏ãÁÄâÔºåÂ∏ÇÊ∞ëÂá∫ÁèæÊê∂Ë≥ºÊÅêÊÖå„ÄÇ1984Âπ¥ÔºåÊ≠∑Âè≤ÊÇ†‰πÖÁöÑËã±Ë≥áÂÖ¨Âè∏ÊÄ°ÂíåÈõÜÂúòÔºåÂ∞áË®ªÂÜäÂú∞ÈªûÁî±È¶ôÊ∏ØÈÅ∑ÂæÄÁôæÊÖïÈÅîÔºåÂèçÊò†ÂêÑÊñπÂ∞çÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂâçÊôØ‰∏çÊä±Ê®ÇËßÄ„ÄÇ

                                            1. The ‚Äú1997‚Äù Question and the 1980s: Sino-British Power Struggles and Hong Kong‚Äôs Responses

                                              Amid uncertainty about Hong Kong‚Äôs post-1997 future, emigration became a form of silent protest for many residents lacking confidence in the handover. However, a significant portion of Hong Kong‚Äôs population either could not or chose not to leave. Among those staying were individuals active in the social and student movements of the 1970s and 1980s, who often held a more positive outlook toward the handover.

                                              These individuals supported electoral participation in District Board and Urban Council elections in 1982 and 1983, advocating for representative government. Simultaneously, they expressed strong nationalist sentiments. For example, the political group Meeting Point‚Äîfounded by ‚Äúsocialist‚Äù student activists from the 1970s‚Äîreleased a proposal in January 1983, Suggestions for Hong Kong‚Äôs Future, emphasizing that Hong Kong was an ‚Äúinseparable part of China‚Äù while also asserting that recognizing China did not equate to endorsing its current regime.

                                              In September 1982, after British Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher‚Äôs visit to Beijing, dozens of university students protested at the airport, displaying banners rejecting the validity of the ‚ÄúThree Unequal Treaties.‚Äù The Hong Kong University Students‚Äô Union wrote to Zhao Ziyang, advocating for ‚Äúdemocratic governance by the people of Hong Kong‚Äù and demanding universal suffrage for Hong Kong‚Äôs local government and chief executive. In May of the same year, Zhao responded positively, affirming that Hong Kong‚Äôs future political system would be democratized, aligning with their call for ‚Äúdemocratic governance.‚Äù

                                              Supporters of this vision were later categorized as the ‚ÄúDemocratic Reunification‚Äù camp, embracing the handover with optimism.

                                              ‚Äî

                                              1. From the 1989 Democratic Movement to the Tiananmen Square Massacre: Sino-British-Hong Kong Relations

                                                (Carroll 2007: 191‚Äì194; Law Wing Sang 2014: 130‚Äì131)

                                                The ‚ÄúDemocratic Reunification‚Äù vision faced setbacks during the ‚Äú1988 Campaign for Genuine Elections.‚Äù Due to Chinese government opposition, the British delayed introducing direct elections to the Legislative Council until 1991. However, the eruption of the 1989 Tiananmen Square protests reinvigorated hope among Hong Kong‚Äôs ‚ÄúDemocratic Reunification‚Äù supporters and those unable or unwilling to emigrate. Many believed that a democratized China would create greater space for Hong Kong‚Äôs own democratization.

                                                Hong Kong residents showed overwhelming solidarity with the 1989 student movement in Beijing. On May 21, 1989, a million people marched in Hong Kong for the first time, expressing concern over Beijing‚Äôs declaration of martial law. However, the situation drastically worsened, culminating in the violent crackdown on June 4, which triggered another mass protest involving a million people in Hong Kong.

                                                The Tiananmen Massacre shattered any remaining confidence in China‚Äôs reforms, plunging Hong Kong into despair. The fear of ‚ÄúBeijing today, Hong Kong tomorrow‚Äù fueled a renewed wave of emigration, with over 60,000 residents leaving annually between 1990 and 1992.

                                                Under the shadow of June 4, the Hong Kong government launched the ‚ÄúHong Kong Is My Home‚Äù campaign, urging Britain to grant permanent residency to 3.25 million Hong Kong British Nationals or help other residents emigrate. Executive Councilor Lydia Dunn emphasized the gravity of transferring British citizens to ‚Äúa regime that had shown no hesitation in suppressing its people with tanks and force.‚Äù

                                                In response, British Foreign Secretary Geoffrey Howe visited Hong Kong in July 1989 but offered residency to only 50,000 qualified individuals and their families under the British Nationality Selection Scheme. The program, formalized in the British Nationality (Hong Kong) Act in December, primarily benefited civil servants and professionals deemed contributors to Hong Kong society, though applications fell short of expectations.

                                                The massacre also plunged Sino-British relations to a low point. Beijing expressed strong dissatisfaction with Hong Kong‚Äôs support for the democratic movement and its role in helping activists escape, fearing Hong Kong could become a base for subversion. New General Secretary Jiang Zemin cautioned against ‚Äúinterference‚Äù in July 1989, while the People‚Äôs Daily accused prominent Hong Kong activists, such as Martin Lee and Szeto Wah, of engaging in subversive activities. Both were removed from the Basic Law Drafting Committee, and Beijing demanded the dissolution of the Hong Kong Alliance in Support of Patriotic Democratic Movements of China.

                                                To reassure Hong Kong‚Äôs public and address international concerns, Governor David Wilson announced plans to introduce a Bill of Rights, aligning with international conventions on civil, political, economic, social, and cultural rights. Beijing publicly opposed the move, claiming it was unnecessary, weakened public security, and contradicted the Basic Law. Nevertheless, the Hong Kong Bill of Rights Ordinance passed its third reading in the Legislative Council in June 1991.

                                                The Tiananmen Massacre left China diplomatically isolated, with Western nations imposing sanctions under a narrative of ‚Äúcontaining China.‚Äù The uncertainty surrounding China‚Äôs reform trajectory further complicated its future. Britain‚Äôs changing China policy in the 1990s also reflected the implications of Hong Kong‚Äôs situation on broader Sino-British relations.

                                                ‚Äî

                                                Impact on Hong Kong‚Äôs Identity and Relations with China

                                                The massacre had a nuanced impact on Hong Kong‚Äôs identity. On the one hand, it pushed many residents, including former pro-China democrats, to emigrate. On the other, Hong Kong‚Äôs active role in the democratic movement deepened many residents‚Äô sense of Chinese identity, binding Hong Kong‚Äôs fate to China‚Äôs future.

                                                The annual Tiananmen vigils in Hong Kong, including those held post-1997, reflected this complex relationship. The Hong Kong Alliance‚Äôs slogan, ‚ÄúSeek accountability for the massacre, build a democratic China,‚Äù exemplifies this connection.

                                                Scholars, such as Law Wing Sang, have described Hong Kong‚Äôs Tiananmen commemorations as a form of ‚Äúdissenting Chinese nationalism,‚Äù portraying Hong Kong as a critical internal critic of China. While this approach occasionally leaned into a ‚ÄúGreater China‚Äù sentimentalism, it also allowed Hong Kong to reassess its cultural subjectivity and relationship with Chinese nationalism in the 1990s and beyond.

Social Hostility toward Christianity

Hong Kong

Historical Period: Before 1990 (with a focus on the decade from 1980 to 1989. As background, briefly mention significant laws and events from earlier periods, particularly those that had a major impact on the circumstances of this decade.)

Researcher Name: Xing Fuzeng

      1. Geopolitical environment and diplomatic relations

        ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

        1. The Executive Council‚Äôs Policy on Land Use by Religious Organizations (1981)

          (References: Christian Weekly 1981; Message 1981; Christian Times 1996)

          In 1973, Governor Murray MacLehose launched the New Town Development Plan to address rapid population growth. Tsuen Wan, Sha Tin, and Tuen Mun were the first new towns, followed by Tai Po, Fanling, Sheung Shui, and Yuen Long in the late 1970s as the second-generation new towns. When the Hong Kong Christian Council reviewed the planning blueprint for Sha Tin New Town, they discovered no designated ‚Äúreligious land use‚Äù areas. Churches could only acquire land at market prices, making it difficult to afford land for building places of worship due to rising property prices.

          The Hong Kong Christian Council, along with the Catholic Diocese of Hong Kong, engaged in multiple negotiations with the government. In 1979, Rev. Kwok Nai Hung (Secretary-General of the Christian Council), Bishop John Gilbert Hind of the Anglican Church, and Cardinal John Baptist Wu of the Catholic Church reached an agreement with Chief Secretary Jack Cater. In March 1981, the Hong Kong government drafted a new policy proposal and consulted with religious organizations. On May 12, the Executive Council officially approved the policy, which allowed religious organizations providing social services to use social service centers for divine worship or clergy residences at no premium under the following conditions:

          1. Religious activities must occupy no more than one floor, and the space must also serve community activities compatible with the religious functions.

            1. Clergy residences must not exceed 200 square meters and should relate directly to the social service activities of the center.

              Additionally, the Executive Council permitted religious organizations to use subsidized school halls for religious purposes. This policy enabled many churches in the 1980s to expand rapidly in new towns by establishing social services and schools.

              ‚Äî

              1. Code of Aid for Secondary Schools and Primary Schools (1983)

                The 1983 Code of Aid for Secondary Schools and Code of Aid for Primary Schools included two provisions regarding religion:

                1. Religious education time was limited to 1.5 hours per week. Any additional time could not be counted as part of the regular curriculum.

                  1. Schools were prohibited from compelling students to receive religious instruction or participate in religious rituals and were required to provide alternative arrangements for those who opted out. Students had to be informed of this rule at the beginning of each academic year.

                    ‚Äî

                    1. Provisions on Religion in the Sino-British Joint Declaration (1984)

                      (Joint Declaration, 1984)

                      In 1984, the governments of China and the United Kingdom reached an agreement on Hong Kong‚Äôs future after 1997 and signed the Joint Declaration. Article 3 outlined China‚Äôs basic policies for Hong Kong, stating that the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region would safeguard freedoms and rights, including those of religion. Annex I further specified:

                      • Religious organizations and believers could maintain ties with counterparts elsewhere. Religious schools, hospitals, and welfare institutions could continue to operate.

                        • Relations between Hong Kong‚Äôs religious organizations and their counterparts in mainland China would be based on mutual independence, non-interference, and mutual respect (the ‚Äúthree mutual principles‚Äù).

                          • Educational autonomy for institutions, including those run by religious and social organizations, would be preserved, and they could recruit staff and select teaching materials freely.

                            These provisions established basic religious freedoms for Hong Kong after 1997, ensuring that religious organizations could maintain international connections, continue operating educational and welfare services, and enjoy institutional autonomy.

                            ‚Äî

                            1. Drafting and Consultation of the Basic Law and Christianity (1985)

                              (Xing Fuzeng, 2018: 207-209)

                              In September 1984, the Sino-British Joint Declaration was signed, affirming China‚Äôs sovereignty over Hong Kong from July 1, 1997. Following the declaration, China launched the drafting of the Basic Law, establishing a Drafting Committee and a Consultative Committee in 1985, which included representatives from the religious community.

                              The Anglican Bishop Peter Kwong and Buddhist leader Sik Kok Kwong were among the drafters. Bishop Kwong, upon joining the drafting committee, expressed his focus on issues such as religious freedom and the continuation of religious participation in social and educational services. The six major religions in Hong Kong appointed representatives to the consultative committee, providing a platform for religious perspectives on the Basic Law.

                              Recognizing the need for an independent Christian voice, the Hong Kong Christian Council established the ‚ÄúHong Kong Christian Concern for the Basic Law Committee‚Äù in 1984, offering a unified platform for diverse denominations to address issues arising from the drafting process.

                              ‚Äî

                              1. Should the Basic Law Include Provisions on ‚ÄúReligious Policy‚Äù? (1986-1987)

                                (Xing Fuzeng, 2018: 246-248)

                                In April 1986, the second plenary meeting of the Basic Law Drafting Committee proposed adding ‚ÄúReligious Policy‚Äù to Chapter VI of the outline. This sparked concerns within Hong Kong‚Äôs religious community. Some argued that having a ‚Äúreligious policy‚Äù was characteristic of authoritarian states, leading to fears of potential government interference in religious affairs.

                                While some Christian leaders advocated for retaining existing ‚Äúprivileges‚Äù granted to religious organizations under colonial policies, others emphasized that constitutional guarantees of religious freedom should not include administrative privileges. In 1987, after extensive debate, the drafting committee decided to remove the controversial ‚Äúreligious policy‚Äù term, opting instead for ‚Äúreligious issues.‚Äù

                                This controversy highlighted differing opinions on the relationship between religion and government in post-1997 Hong Kong. It reflected the tension between maintaining colonial-era privileges for religious organizations and upholding the principle of religious freedom without administrative favoritism.

                                1. Controversy Over the Christian Delegation‚Äôs Visit to Beijing

                                  (Xing Fuzeng, 2018: 180-185)

                                  The planned visit of a Hong Kong Christian delegation to Beijing from September 5 to 9 sparked significant attention within the church community.

                                  Rev. Lam Yu Shing acknowledged in Christian Weekly that ‚Äúsome believers remain entangled in past nightmares,‚Äù adding, ‚Äúwhile various groups are organizing visits to Beijing, some church leaders hesitate.‚Äù Upon returning to Hong Kong, Yu Dat San shared his observations and emotions in writing. He candidly noted: ‚ÄúMany brothers and sisters strongly oppose any interaction or dialogue between Hong Kong churches and the Chinese Communist government out of fear of becoming targets of united front strategies. From historical lessons, the term ‚Äòunited front‚Äô undoubtedly evokes unease and concern. No wonder some believers tremble at its mention.‚Äù He further pointed out: ‚ÄúFor some members of our delegation, accepting this invitation was no easy decision. Concerns ranged from doubts about the effectiveness of the visit to fears that the delegation would become a tool for the Religious Affairs Bureau and the Three-Self Patriotic Movement. A bigger worry was the potential rejection of this action by church members.‚Äù

                                  To address these concerns, evangelical members of the delegation organized a sharing session on August 18 at Hebron Church. Prominent figures such as Rev. Stephen Tong, Philip Chien (Student Evangelical Fellowship), and Ernest Choi (Breakthrough) expressed their views. The Olive Tree noted particular interest in Rev. Tong, who rarely commented on Chinese politics and church issues. At the session, some attendees questioned the ‚Äúunited front‚Äù implications of the visit and whether it might undermine the diversity within Hong Kong churches. These issues were addressed by the delegation members.

                                  Philip Chien emphasized the need for trust and spiritual support from the church: ‚ÄúWe need courage, wisdom, and the Holy Spirit‚Äôs guidance to make this visit a genuine opportunity for dialogue between the church and the government.‚Äù Ernest Choi admitted his initial hesitation upon receiving the invitation, describing his inner conflict.

                                  Rev. Tong highlighted his belief in God‚Äôs sovereignty over history, stating that the church must adapt wisely to changes. He shared that, upon receiving the invitation, he felt compelled to voice the church‚Äôs stance on religious freedom, saying, ‚ÄúHong Kong citizens are expressing their views on Hong Kong‚Äôs future; the Christian community also has a responsibility to speak out.‚Äù He dismissed accusations of futility, emphasizing a dynamic perspective on China‚Äôs evolving policies. He acknowledged the potential for practical religious freedom in Hong Kong under the Chinese Communist Party‚Äôs shifting economic policies.

                                  Rev. Tong‚Äôs decision to accept the invitation marked a significant departure from his typically reserved approach to Chinese and political matters. However, on the day of departure, he dramatically withdrew from the trip, reflecting the immense pressures he faced.

                                  At the time, many Hong Kong and North American Chinese churches maintained a ‚Äúno reconciliation‚Äù stance toward the Chinese Communist Party and the Three-Self Movement, branding any interaction as betrayal. For instance, North American pastor Wang Guoxian warned of potential infiltration by Communist Party members like Li Chuwen, who had posed as an underground church member. Wang criticized the delegation, asserting that it was a political tool to placate Hong Kong citizens and project a facade of openness internationally.

                                  ‚Äî

                                  1. Controversy Over Preferential Hiring of Christian Teachers in Government-Subsidized Schools

                                    Starting in the 1970s, the Hong Kong government significantly invested in education to address shortages of school places, implementing six and nine years of free education in 1971 and 1978, respectively. Many Christian organizations accepted government subsidies to establish schools, incorporating Bible studies into their curricula. Evangelical organizations often prioritized evangelism among students, leading to job advertisements favoring Christian applicants.

                                    By the 1980s, this hiring practice drew criticism, with claims that it violated the principles of public fund usage. In 1986, the South China Morning Post published a letter alleging discriminatory practices during interviews, such as questioning applicants‚Äô biblical knowledge and church involvement. Critics argued this amounted to discrimination against non-Christians.

                                    In response, leaders like Cheung Tsz Kwong defended the practice, emphasizing the freedom of choice in Hong Kong‚Äôs diverse educational landscape. He argued that schools with religious missions required teachers who shared their faith to uphold their values. Critics countered that using public funds for religious purposes was discriminatory and incompatible with public accountability.

                                    In 1996, the establishment of the Equal Opportunities Commission led to regulations prohibiting discriminatory language like ‚ÄúChristian preferred‚Äù in job advertisements.

                                    ‚Äî

                                    1. Controversy Over The Last Temptation of Christ (1988)

                                      (Christian Weekly, 1988; Wah Kiu Yat Po, 1988; Ta Kung Pao, 1988; Liu Dafa, 1988)

                                      In November 1988, the film The Last Temptation of Christ was classified as Category III (restricted to adults over 18) and screened in Hong Kong. Due to its portrayal of Jesus facing human temptations, the film provoked widespread controversy among Christian communities globally.

                                      On November 7, the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union sent letters to the Chief Secretary‚Äôs Office, legislative council members, and the Film Censorship Office, requesting a ban on the film to protect the sanctity of Christ‚Äôs image. The Union also distributed letter templates to 249 member churches, urging them to petition the authorities. A special task force was formed, declaring, ‚ÄúHong Kong believers must not remain silent in defending the dignity of faith.‚Äù

                                      While many religious groups joined the call for a ban, others advocated a rational response, emphasizing freedom of speech and artistic expression in Hong Kong. Outside a Wan Chai theater, about 20 church members protested, distributing leaflets urging the public not to watch the film.

                                      This incident highlighted tensions between religious advocacy and freedom of expression, reflecting broader debates about moral and social values in Hong Kong.

                                      1. The ‚Äú1997‚Äù Question and 1980s Hong Kong: Sino-British Power Struggles

                                        (Carroll 2007: 179‚Äì187; Xu Cheng‚Äôen 2023: 246‚Äì254; Liu Shuyong 2016: 319‚Äì323)

                                        In 1980s Hong Kong, the question of ‚Äú1997‚Äù dominated the decade, centering on Sino-British negotiations, agreements over Hong Kong‚Äôs future, and the power struggle regarding arrangements during the transitional period before the promulgation of the Basic Law in 1991. Events such as the 1989 Beijing Student Movement and the subsequent Tiananmen Square Massacre further reshaped the triangular relationship between China, Britain, and Hong Kong.

                                        In 1979, Governor Murray MacLehose visited Beijing and learned of China‚Äôs decision to reclaim Hong Kong in 1997. Britain responded with preparations:

                                        1. In 1980, the Hong Kong government issued the Green Paper on Local Administration, followed by the White Paper on Local Administration and the District Boards Ordinance in 1981. These measures led to the first District Board elections in 1982 and Municipal Council elections in 1983.

                                          1. The British Nationality Act 1981 stripped Hong Kong residents of their right of abode in the UK.

                                            Although Sino-British negotiations on Hong Kong‚Äôs future had not formally begun, Britain‚Äôs reforms in local governance during the postwar period were likely intended to pave the way for representative democracy.

                                            In September 1982, British Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher visited China, initiating official negotiations on Hong Kong‚Äôs future. China insisted that the talks were a Sino-British matter, excluding Hong Kong‚Äôs direct participation. Britain‚Äôs proposal for ‚Äúsovereignty for administration‚Äù was rejected by China, which refused to recognize the Treaty of Nanking (1842), the Convention of Peking (1860), or the Convention for the Extension of Hong Kong Territory (1898). By late 1983, China warned that it would unilaterally announce plans to reclaim Hong Kong if no agreement was reached by October 1, 1984, pressuring Britain to shift its focus from sovereignty to ensuring stability post-handover.

                                            In September 1984, representatives from China and Britain reached an agreement and drafted the Sino-British Joint Declaration, which was formally signed in December by Thatcher and Chinese Premier Zhao Ziyang. The declaration stipulated that Hong Kong would be returned to China on July 1, 1997, and a Special Administrative Region (SAR) would be established with a high degree of autonomy, excluding foreign affairs and defense. Hong Kong‚Äôs existing social and economic systems and lifestyle would remain unchanged.

                                            In 1985, following the exchange of ratifications for the Joint Declaration, Hong Kong officially entered its transitional period, with preparations for the 1997 handover, including the drafting of the Basic Law. The drafting committee, established in June 1985, included members from China and Hong Kong, and a consultative committee was set up in December of the same year. Although China would officially reclaim sovereignty in 1997, Chinese influence was already apparent during the transitional period, with Beijing launching united front campaigns targeting various sectors in Hong Kong. The business community, playing the role of ‚Äúlocal collaborators,‚Äù emerged as a counterforce to the pro-democracy camp.

                                            During the late stages of the Sino-British negotiations, the Hong Kong government released the Green Paper on Representative Government in 1984, proposing a gradual move towards a system grounded in public opinion and accountability to Hong Kong residents. The subsequent White Paper proposed 24 indirectly elected members for the Legislative Council in 1985, reducing appointed and ex officio members to 22 and 19, respectively. It also mentioned the possibility of introducing directly elected seats in 1988. However, China opposed accelerated democratic reforms, arguing that the transitional period‚Äôs political system must align with the Basic Law.

                                            Under pressure from China, Britain agreed to avoid major political reforms before the promulgation of the Basic Law in 1990. In 1987, the Hong Kong government‚Äôs Green Paper reviewed the development of representative government but deferred the introduction of directly elected seats to 1991 due to societal divisions and concerns about smooth power transitions in 1997.

                                            Throughout the negotiations, Hong Kong‚Äôs voice was largely absent. While many Hong Kong residents preferred British rule post-1997 due to distrust in the Chinese Communist Party, fears of instability similar to post-1949 mainland China fueled skepticism. The 1983 devaluation of the Hong Kong dollar amid stalled Sino-British negotiations and Jardine Matheson‚Äôs relocation to Bermuda in 1984 underscored the uncertainty.

                                            From the onset of Sino-British negotiations to the signing of the Joint Declaration, a wave of emigration swept through Hong Kong. From 1980 to 1986, approximately 20,000 residents emigrated annually. The Tiananmen Square Massacre in 1989 further accelerated this trend, peaking in subsequent years. The 1994 publication of Reluctant Exiles? by the University of Hong Kong explored this emigration phenomenon, highlighting different perspectives through its Chinese title, ‚Äú‰ΩïÂúüÂêæÈÑâ?‚Äù (Where is My Home?).

                                            1980Âπ¥‰ª£ÁöÑÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÔºå„Äå‰πù‰∏É„ÄçÁÑ°ÁñëÊòØÊï¥ÂÄãÂçÅÂπ¥ÁöÑÈóúÈçµË©ûÔºåËÉåÂæåÊ∂âÂèä‰∫Ü‰∏≠Ëã±ÂÖ©ÂúãÂ∞±È¶ôÊ∏ØÂâçÈÄîÂïèÈ°åÁöÑË´áÂà§„ÄÅÂçîË≠∞Ôºå‰ª•ÂèäÂú®Âü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÈ†í‰ΩàÔºà1991ÔºâÂâç‰∏≠Ëã±Â∞±È¶ôÊ∏ØÈÅéÊ∏°ÊúüÂÆâÊéíÁöÑËßíÂäõ„ÄÇÂÖ∂‰∏≠1989Âπ¥Âåó‰∫¨Â≠∏ÈÅãÁöÑÁàÜÁôºÔºå‰ª•Âèä„ÄåÂÖ≠Âõõ„ÄçÂ±†ÊÆ∫ÊÖòÊ°àÂâçÂæåÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁ§æÊúÉÂ∞ç‰∏≠ÂúãÊ∞ë‰∏ªÈÅãÂãïÁöÑÊîØÊè¥Ôºå‰πüÊää‰∏≠Ëã±Ê∏ØÁöÑ‰∏âËßíÈóú‰øÇÊé®ÂêëÊñ∞ÁöÑÊ†ºÂ±Ä„ÄÇ

                                            1979Âπ¥Ê∏ØÁù£È∫•ÁêÜÊµ©Ë®™ÂïèÂåó‰∫¨ÔºåÁü•ÊÇâ‰∏≠ÂÖ±Âú®1997Âπ¥Êî∂ÂõûÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁöÑÊ±∫ÂÆöÂæåÔºåËã±ÂúãÈñãÂßã‰ΩúÂá∫ÈÉ®ÁΩ≤ÔºöÔºà1Ôºâ1980Âπ¥ÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊîøÂ∫úÁôºË°®„ÄäÂú∞ÊñπË°åÊîøÊ®°ÂºèÁ∂†ÁöÆÊõ∏„ÄãÔºåÁøåÂπ¥ÂÖ¨‰Ωà„ÄäÂú∞ÊñπË°åÊîøÁôΩÁöÆÊõ∏„ÄãÂíå„ÄäÂçÄË≠∞ÊúÉÊ¢ù‰æã„ÄãÔºå‰∏¶ÊìöÊ≠§Êñº1982Âπ¥ËàâË°åÈ¶ñÂ±ÜÂçÄË≠∞ÊúÉÈÅ∏Ëàâ„ÄÇ1983Âπ¥ÔºåÂ∏ÇÊîøÂ±Ä‰∫¶ËàâË°åÂàÜÂçÄÈÅ∏ËàâÔºõÔºà2Ôºâ1981Âπ¥ÈÄöÈÅé„ÄäËã±ÂúãÂúãÁ±çÊ≥ï„ÄãÔºåÂâùÂ•™È¶ôÊ∏ØÂ±ÖÊ∞ëÁöÑÂ±ÖËã±Ê¨äÂà©„ÄÇÊòØÊôÇÔºå‰∏≠Ëã±ÂÖ©Âúã‰ªçÊú™Ê≠£ÂºèÂ∞±È¶ôÊ∏ØÂâçÈÄîÂïèÈ°åÈÄ≤Ë°åÁ£ãÂïÜÔºå‰ΩÜËã±ÂúãÂçªËëóÊâãÊé®Ë°åÊà∞ÂæåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂú∞ÊñπË°åÊîøÁöÑÈáçÂ§ßÊîπÈù©ÔºåÁõ∏‰ø°ÊòØÁÇ∫Êó•ÂæåÁôºÂ±ï‰ª£Ë≠∞ÊîøÂà∂‰ΩúÈ†êÂÇô„ÄÇ

                                            Ëá™ÂßãËá≥ÁµÇÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰πù‰∏ÉË´áÂà§ÂèäÂçîË≠∞ÂùáÁî±‰∏≠Ëã±ÂÖ©ÂúãÊâÄ‰∏ªÂ∞éÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∫∫ÁöÑÊÑèË¶ãÁÑ°Ê≥ïÁõ¥Êé•Âú®Ë´áÂà§‰∏ä‰∫à‰ª•Ë°®ÈÅî„ÄÇÈù¢Â∞çÂâçÈÄî„ÄåÂ§ßÈôê„ÄçÔºåÂç≥‰ΩøËã±ÂúãÂú®1981Âπ¥ÈÄöÈÅé„ÄäËã±ÂúãÂúãÁ±çÊ≥ï„ÄãÔºåÊ∏Ø‰∫∫‰ªçÊôÆÈÅçÊúüÊúõ‰πù‰∏ÉÂπ¥ÂæåÁπºÁ∫åÊé•ÂèóËã±ÂúãÁÆ°Ê≤ª„ÄÇ‰∏ªË¶ÅÂéüÂõ†ÔºåÁÑ°ÁñëÊòØÂ∞ç‰∏≠ÂúãÂÖ±Áî¢Èª®Áº∫‰πè‰ø°ÂøÉÔºåÊìîÂøÉ1949Âπ¥‰∏≠ÂÖ±Âª∫ÂúãÂæåÁöÑÂãïÁõ™ÔºåÊúÉÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÈáçÊºî„ÄÇ„Äç1983Âπ¥9ÊúàÔºåÁî±Êñº‰∏≠Ëã±Ë´áÂà§Ê≤íÊúâÈÄ≤Â±ïÔºåÊ∏ØÂÖÉÂåØÂÉπÊÄ•Âäá‰∏ãÁÄâÔºåÂ∏ÇÊ∞ëÂá∫ÁèæÊê∂Ë≥ºÊÅêÊÖå„ÄÇ1984Âπ¥ÔºåÊ≠∑Âè≤ÊÇ†‰πÖÁöÑËã±Ë≥áÂÖ¨Âè∏ÊÄ°ÂíåÈõÜÂúòÔºåÂ∞áË®ªÂÜäÂú∞ÈªûÁî±È¶ôÊ∏ØÈÅ∑ÂæÄÁôæÊÖïÈÅîÔºåÂèçÊò†ÂêÑÊñπÂ∞çÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂâçÊôØ‰∏çÊä±Ê®ÇËßÄ„ÄÇ

                                            1. The ‚Äú1997‚Äù Question and the 1980s: Sino-British Power Struggles and Hong Kong‚Äôs Responses

                                              Amid uncertainty about Hong Kong‚Äôs post-1997 future, emigration became a form of silent protest for many residents lacking confidence in the handover. However, a significant portion of Hong Kong‚Äôs population either could not or chose not to leave. Among those staying were individuals active in the social and student movements of the 1970s and 1980s, who often held a more positive outlook toward the handover.

                                              These individuals supported electoral participation in District Board and Urban Council elections in 1982 and 1983, advocating for representative government. Simultaneously, they expressed strong nationalist sentiments. For example, the political group Meeting Point‚Äîfounded by ‚Äúsocialist‚Äù student activists from the 1970s‚Äîreleased a proposal in January 1983, Suggestions for Hong Kong‚Äôs Future, emphasizing that Hong Kong was an ‚Äúinseparable part of China‚Äù while also asserting that recognizing China did not equate to endorsing its current regime.

                                              In September 1982, after British Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher‚Äôs visit to Beijing, dozens of university students protested at the airport, displaying banners rejecting the validity of the ‚ÄúThree Unequal Treaties.‚Äù The Hong Kong University Students‚Äô Union wrote to Zhao Ziyang, advocating for ‚Äúdemocratic governance by the people of Hong Kong‚Äù and demanding universal suffrage for Hong Kong‚Äôs local government and chief executive. In May of the same year, Zhao responded positively, affirming that Hong Kong‚Äôs future political system would be democratized, aligning with their call for ‚Äúdemocratic governance.‚Äù

                                              Supporters of this vision were later categorized as the ‚ÄúDemocratic Reunification‚Äù camp, embracing the handover with optimism.

                                              ‚Äî

                                              1. From the 1989 Democratic Movement to the Tiananmen Square Massacre: Sino-British-Hong Kong Relations

                                                (Carroll 2007: 191‚Äì194; Law Wing Sang 2014: 130‚Äì131)

                                                The ‚ÄúDemocratic Reunification‚Äù vision faced setbacks during the ‚Äú1988 Campaign for Genuine Elections.‚Äù Due to Chinese government opposition, the British delayed introducing direct elections to the Legislative Council until 1991. However, the eruption of the 1989 Tiananmen Square protests reinvigorated hope among Hong Kong‚Äôs ‚ÄúDemocratic Reunification‚Äù supporters and those unable or unwilling to emigrate. Many believed that a democratized China would create greater space for Hong Kong‚Äôs own democratization.

                                                Hong Kong residents showed overwhelming solidarity with the 1989 student movement in Beijing. On May 21, 1989, a million people marched in Hong Kong for the first time, expressing concern over Beijing‚Äôs declaration of martial law. However, the situation drastically worsened, culminating in the violent crackdown on June 4, which triggered another mass protest involving a million people in Hong Kong.

                                                The Tiananmen Massacre shattered any remaining confidence in China‚Äôs reforms, plunging Hong Kong into despair. The fear of ‚ÄúBeijing today, Hong Kong tomorrow‚Äù fueled a renewed wave of emigration, with over 60,000 residents leaving annually between 1990 and 1992.

                                                Under the shadow of June 4, the Hong Kong government launched the ‚ÄúHong Kong Is My Home‚Äù campaign, urging Britain to grant permanent residency to 3.25 million Hong Kong British Nationals or help other residents emigrate. Executive Councilor Lydia Dunn emphasized the gravity of transferring British citizens to ‚Äúa regime that had shown no hesitation in suppressing its people with tanks and force.‚Äù

                                                In response, British Foreign Secretary Geoffrey Howe visited Hong Kong in July 1989 but offered residency to only 50,000 qualified individuals and their families under the British Nationality Selection Scheme. The program, formalized in the British Nationality (Hong Kong) Act in December, primarily benefited civil servants and professionals deemed contributors to Hong Kong society, though applications fell short of expectations.

                                                The massacre also plunged Sino-British relations to a low point. Beijing expressed strong dissatisfaction with Hong Kong‚Äôs support for the democratic movement and its role in helping activists escape, fearing Hong Kong could become a base for subversion. New General Secretary Jiang Zemin cautioned against ‚Äúinterference‚Äù in July 1989, while the People‚Äôs Daily accused prominent Hong Kong activists, such as Martin Lee and Szeto Wah, of engaging in subversive activities. Both were removed from the Basic Law Drafting Committee, and Beijing demanded the dissolution of the Hong Kong Alliance in Support of Patriotic Democratic Movements of China.

                                                To reassure Hong Kong‚Äôs public and address international concerns, Governor David Wilson announced plans to introduce a Bill of Rights, aligning with international conventions on civil, political, economic, social, and cultural rights. Beijing publicly opposed the move, claiming it was unnecessary, weakened public security, and contradicted the Basic Law. Nevertheless, the Hong Kong Bill of Rights Ordinance passed its third reading in the Legislative Council in June 1991.

                                                The Tiananmen Massacre left China diplomatically isolated, with Western nations imposing sanctions under a narrative of ‚Äúcontaining China.‚Äù The uncertainty surrounding China‚Äôs reform trajectory further complicated its future. Britain‚Äôs changing China policy in the 1990s also reflected the implications of Hong Kong‚Äôs situation on broader Sino-British relations.

                                                ‚Äî

                                                Impact on Hong Kong‚Äôs Identity and Relations with China

                                                The massacre had a nuanced impact on Hong Kong‚Äôs identity. On the one hand, it pushed many residents, including former pro-China democrats, to emigrate. On the other, Hong Kong‚Äôs active role in the democratic movement deepened many residents‚Äô sense of Chinese identity, binding Hong Kong‚Äôs fate to China‚Äôs future.

                                                The annual Tiananmen vigils in Hong Kong, including those held post-1997, reflected this complex relationship. The Hong Kong Alliance‚Äôs slogan, ‚ÄúSeek accountability for the massacre, build a democratic China,‚Äù exemplifies this connection.

                                                Scholars, such as Law Wing Sang, have described Hong Kong‚Äôs Tiananmen commemorations as a form of ‚Äúdissenting Chinese nationalism,‚Äù portraying Hong Kong as a critical internal critic of China. While this approach occasionally leaned into a ‚ÄúGreater China‚Äù sentimentalism, it also allowed Hong Kong to reassess its cultural subjectivity and relationship with Chinese nationalism in the 1990s and beyond.

Geopolitical Dynamics and International Relationships

Hong Kong

Historical Period: Before 1990 (with a focus on the decade from 1980 to 1989. As background, briefly mention significant laws and events from earlier periods, particularly those that had a major impact on the circumstances of this decade.)

Researcher Name: Xing Fuzeng

        ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

        1. The Executive Council‚Äôs Policy on Land Use by Religious Organizations (1981)

          (References: Christian Weekly 1981; Message 1981; Christian Times 1996)

          In 1973, Governor Murray MacLehose launched the New Town Development Plan to address rapid population growth. Tsuen Wan, Sha Tin, and Tuen Mun were the first new towns, followed by Tai Po, Fanling, Sheung Shui, and Yuen Long in the late 1970s as the second-generation new towns. When the Hong Kong Christian Council reviewed the planning blueprint for Sha Tin New Town, they discovered no designated ‚Äúreligious land use‚Äù areas. Churches could only acquire land at market prices, making it difficult to afford land for building places of worship due to rising property prices.

          The Hong Kong Christian Council, along with the Catholic Diocese of Hong Kong, engaged in multiple negotiations with the government. In 1979, Rev. Kwok Nai Hung (Secretary-General of the Christian Council), Bishop John Gilbert Hind of the Anglican Church, and Cardinal John Baptist Wu of the Catholic Church reached an agreement with Chief Secretary Jack Cater. In March 1981, the Hong Kong government drafted a new policy proposal and consulted with religious organizations. On May 12, the Executive Council officially approved the policy, which allowed religious organizations providing social services to use social service centers for divine worship or clergy residences at no premium under the following conditions:

          1. Religious activities must occupy no more than one floor, and the space must also serve community activities compatible with the religious functions.

            1. Clergy residences must not exceed 200 square meters and should relate directly to the social service activities of the center.

              Additionally, the Executive Council permitted religious organizations to use subsidized school halls for religious purposes. This policy enabled many churches in the 1980s to expand rapidly in new towns by establishing social services and schools.

              ‚Äî

              1. Code of Aid for Secondary Schools and Primary Schools (1983)

                The 1983 Code of Aid for Secondary Schools and Code of Aid for Primary Schools included two provisions regarding religion:

                1. Religious education time was limited to 1.5 hours per week. Any additional time could not be counted as part of the regular curriculum.

                  1. Schools were prohibited from compelling students to receive religious instruction or participate in religious rituals and were required to provide alternative arrangements for those who opted out. Students had to be informed of this rule at the beginning of each academic year.

                    ‚Äî

                    1. Provisions on Religion in the Sino-British Joint Declaration (1984)

                      (Joint Declaration, 1984)

                      In 1984, the governments of China and the United Kingdom reached an agreement on Hong Kong‚Äôs future after 1997 and signed the Joint Declaration. Article 3 outlined China‚Äôs basic policies for Hong Kong, stating that the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region would safeguard freedoms and rights, including those of religion. Annex I further specified:

                      • Religious organizations and believers could maintain ties with counterparts elsewhere. Religious schools, hospitals, and welfare institutions could continue to operate.

                        • Relations between Hong Kong‚Äôs religious organizations and their counterparts in mainland China would be based on mutual independence, non-interference, and mutual respect (the ‚Äúthree mutual principles‚Äù).

                          • Educational autonomy for institutions, including those run by religious and social organizations, would be preserved, and they could recruit staff and select teaching materials freely.

                            These provisions established basic religious freedoms for Hong Kong after 1997, ensuring that religious organizations could maintain international connections, continue operating educational and welfare services, and enjoy institutional autonomy.

                            ‚Äî

                            1. Drafting and Consultation of the Basic Law and Christianity (1985)

                              (Xing Fuzeng, 2018: 207-209)

                              In September 1984, the Sino-British Joint Declaration was signed, affirming China‚Äôs sovereignty over Hong Kong from July 1, 1997. Following the declaration, China launched the drafting of the Basic Law, establishing a Drafting Committee and a Consultative Committee in 1985, which included representatives from the religious community.

                              The Anglican Bishop Peter Kwong and Buddhist leader Sik Kok Kwong were among the drafters. Bishop Kwong, upon joining the drafting committee, expressed his focus on issues such as religious freedom and the continuation of religious participation in social and educational services. The six major religions in Hong Kong appointed representatives to the consultative committee, providing a platform for religious perspectives on the Basic Law.

                              Recognizing the need for an independent Christian voice, the Hong Kong Christian Council established the ‚ÄúHong Kong Christian Concern for the Basic Law Committee‚Äù in 1984, offering a unified platform for diverse denominations to address issues arising from the drafting process.

                              ‚Äî

                              1. Should the Basic Law Include Provisions on ‚ÄúReligious Policy‚Äù? (1986-1987)

                                (Xing Fuzeng, 2018: 246-248)

                                In April 1986, the second plenary meeting of the Basic Law Drafting Committee proposed adding ‚ÄúReligious Policy‚Äù to Chapter VI of the outline. This sparked concerns within Hong Kong‚Äôs religious community. Some argued that having a ‚Äúreligious policy‚Äù was characteristic of authoritarian states, leading to fears of potential government interference in religious affairs.

                                While some Christian leaders advocated for retaining existing ‚Äúprivileges‚Äù granted to religious organizations under colonial policies, others emphasized that constitutional guarantees of religious freedom should not include administrative privileges. In 1987, after extensive debate, the drafting committee decided to remove the controversial ‚Äúreligious policy‚Äù term, opting instead for ‚Äúreligious issues.‚Äù

                                This controversy highlighted differing opinions on the relationship between religion and government in post-1997 Hong Kong. It reflected the tension between maintaining colonial-era privileges for religious organizations and upholding the principle of religious freedom without administrative favoritism.

                                1. Controversy Over the Christian Delegation‚Äôs Visit to Beijing

                                  (Xing Fuzeng, 2018: 180-185)

                                  The planned visit of a Hong Kong Christian delegation to Beijing from September 5 to 9 sparked significant attention within the church community.

                                  Rev. Lam Yu Shing acknowledged in Christian Weekly that ‚Äúsome believers remain entangled in past nightmares,‚Äù adding, ‚Äúwhile various groups are organizing visits to Beijing, some church leaders hesitate.‚Äù Upon returning to Hong Kong, Yu Dat San shared his observations and emotions in writing. He candidly noted: ‚ÄúMany brothers and sisters strongly oppose any interaction or dialogue between Hong Kong churches and the Chinese Communist government out of fear of becoming targets of united front strategies. From historical lessons, the term ‚Äòunited front‚Äô undoubtedly evokes unease and concern. No wonder some believers tremble at its mention.‚Äù He further pointed out: ‚ÄúFor some members of our delegation, accepting this invitation was no easy decision. Concerns ranged from doubts about the effectiveness of the visit to fears that the delegation would become a tool for the Religious Affairs Bureau and the Three-Self Patriotic Movement. A bigger worry was the potential rejection of this action by church members.‚Äù

                                  To address these concerns, evangelical members of the delegation organized a sharing session on August 18 at Hebron Church. Prominent figures such as Rev. Stephen Tong, Philip Chien (Student Evangelical Fellowship), and Ernest Choi (Breakthrough) expressed their views. The Olive Tree noted particular interest in Rev. Tong, who rarely commented on Chinese politics and church issues. At the session, some attendees questioned the ‚Äúunited front‚Äù implications of the visit and whether it might undermine the diversity within Hong Kong churches. These issues were addressed by the delegation members.

                                  Philip Chien emphasized the need for trust and spiritual support from the church: ‚ÄúWe need courage, wisdom, and the Holy Spirit‚Äôs guidance to make this visit a genuine opportunity for dialogue between the church and the government.‚Äù Ernest Choi admitted his initial hesitation upon receiving the invitation, describing his inner conflict.

                                  Rev. Tong highlighted his belief in God‚Äôs sovereignty over history, stating that the church must adapt wisely to changes. He shared that, upon receiving the invitation, he felt compelled to voice the church‚Äôs stance on religious freedom, saying, ‚ÄúHong Kong citizens are expressing their views on Hong Kong‚Äôs future; the Christian community also has a responsibility to speak out.‚Äù He dismissed accusations of futility, emphasizing a dynamic perspective on China‚Äôs evolving policies. He acknowledged the potential for practical religious freedom in Hong Kong under the Chinese Communist Party‚Äôs shifting economic policies.

                                  Rev. Tong‚Äôs decision to accept the invitation marked a significant departure from his typically reserved approach to Chinese and political matters. However, on the day of departure, he dramatically withdrew from the trip, reflecting the immense pressures he faced.

                                  At the time, many Hong Kong and North American Chinese churches maintained a ‚Äúno reconciliation‚Äù stance toward the Chinese Communist Party and the Three-Self Movement, branding any interaction as betrayal. For instance, North American pastor Wang Guoxian warned of potential infiltration by Communist Party members like Li Chuwen, who had posed as an underground church member. Wang criticized the delegation, asserting that it was a political tool to placate Hong Kong citizens and project a facade of openness internationally.

                                  ‚Äî

                                  1. Controversy Over Preferential Hiring of Christian Teachers in Government-Subsidized Schools

                                    Starting in the 1970s, the Hong Kong government significantly invested in education to address shortages of school places, implementing six and nine years of free education in 1971 and 1978, respectively. Many Christian organizations accepted government subsidies to establish schools, incorporating Bible studies into their curricula. Evangelical organizations often prioritized evangelism among students, leading to job advertisements favoring Christian applicants.

                                    By the 1980s, this hiring practice drew criticism, with claims that it violated the principles of public fund usage. In 1986, the South China Morning Post published a letter alleging discriminatory practices during interviews, such as questioning applicants‚Äô biblical knowledge and church involvement. Critics argued this amounted to discrimination against non-Christians.

                                    In response, leaders like Cheung Tsz Kwong defended the practice, emphasizing the freedom of choice in Hong Kong‚Äôs diverse educational landscape. He argued that schools with religious missions required teachers who shared their faith to uphold their values. Critics countered that using public funds for religious purposes was discriminatory and incompatible with public accountability.

                                    In 1996, the establishment of the Equal Opportunities Commission led to regulations prohibiting discriminatory language like ‚ÄúChristian preferred‚Äù in job advertisements.

                                    ‚Äî

                                    1. Controversy Over The Last Temptation of Christ (1988)

                                      (Christian Weekly, 1988; Wah Kiu Yat Po, 1988; Ta Kung Pao, 1988; Liu Dafa, 1988)

                                      In November 1988, the film The Last Temptation of Christ was classified as Category III (restricted to adults over 18) and screened in Hong Kong. Due to its portrayal of Jesus facing human temptations, the film provoked widespread controversy among Christian communities globally.

                                      On November 7, the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union sent letters to the Chief Secretary‚Äôs Office, legislative council members, and the Film Censorship Office, requesting a ban on the film to protect the sanctity of Christ‚Äôs image. The Union also distributed letter templates to 249 member churches, urging them to petition the authorities. A special task force was formed, declaring, ‚ÄúHong Kong believers must not remain silent in defending the dignity of faith.‚Äù

                                      While many religious groups joined the call for a ban, others advocated a rational response, emphasizing freedom of speech and artistic expression in Hong Kong. Outside a Wan Chai theater, about 20 church members protested, distributing leaflets urging the public not to watch the film.

                                      This incident highlighted tensions between religious advocacy and freedom of expression, reflecting broader debates about moral and social values in Hong Kong.
